  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3471297168?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inkspill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inkspill" href="/user/show/79575646-inkspill">Inkspill</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3471297168" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8558831804890470033"><i>more 3.5 stars</i><br/><br/>The drama follows on from Shakespeare’s Julius Caesar, but it’s a complete change of pace. This is set four years later, there is a rift between Marcus Antony and Octavius. The welfare of Rome is overseen by both of them and a third Lepidus, whom Shakespeare pays the least attention to. <br/><br/>I’m not sure what to make of this play – in the background there’s a rumble of rift and war when it’s driven by love. I think one of the problems with this play is that there are too many minor char</span>
  <span id="freeText8558831804890470033" style="display:none"><i>more 3.5 stars</i><br /><br />The drama follows on from Shakespeare’s Julius Caesar, but it’s a complete change of pace. This is set four years later, there is a rift between Marcus Antony and Octavius. The welfare of Rome is overseen by both of them and a third Lepidus, whom Shakespeare pays the least attention to. <br /><br />I’m not sure what to make of this play – in the background there’s a rumble of rift and war when it’s driven by love. I think one of the problems with this play is that there are too many minor characters to keep a track off; by the time I’ve grasped their contribution to the drama they’ve disappeared. This kept distracting me from the tension that was building up.<br /><br />I was also surprised how all of Cleopatra’s being was about wanting to Antony prove he loved her, being a queen, this got me wondering if Elizabeth I of England attended the performance? A quick google search told me this play was first performed in 1607, so the answer is no as her reign ended in 1603 and James I (or James VI of Scotland) is King of England of now. <br /><br />I enjoyed reading the play for its poetry but found it lacked the same tension and drama of Julius Caesar, the most interesting was the change in how Antony is regarded when he becomes smitten with Cleopatra. Thinking about it now, it’s tragic in how he couldn’t make up his mind of whether he wanted a life with Cleopatra or regain his honour amongst his countrymen; when his wife dies, so he’s free to marry Cleopatra, he marries Octavius’s sister to prove his loyalty to him. It was their fiery scenes and languishing ones with Cleopatra that overshadowed the drama of Antony’s inner conflict. </span>

<div id="review_1661229901" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1661229901" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1661229901?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199535781" href="/book/show/3334189-anthony-and-cleopatra">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=bard">bard</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=my-faves">my-faves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=toga-time-tomes">toga-time-tomes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1661229901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9301867806824215783">Mark it thus...<br/>The Bard meets the greatest love story of all time.<br/>And the result is -- hours later -- after epic power plays, wrenching loyalty shifts in love and politics, the movements of nations; love won, lost, won and lost again -- after anger, vengeance, recrimination, betrayal and tears -- the tears of the players, and ours; and the final extinguishment of love's embers, we are spent and satisfied and elated and, again, moved by the work of an incomparable genius.<br/><br/>I devoted myself to an <i>An</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9301867806824215783" style="display:none">Mark it thus...<br />The Bard meets the greatest love story of all time.<br />And the result is -- hours later -- after epic power plays, wrenching loyalty shifts in love and politics, the movements of nations; love won, lost, won and lost again -- after anger, vengeance, recrimination, betrayal and tears -- the tears of the players, and ours; and the final extinguishment of love's embers, we are spent and satisfied and elated and, again, moved by the work of an incomparable genius.<br /><br />I devoted myself to an <i>Antony and Cleopatra</i> marathon, party, crash-study session -- call it what you will -- for seven hours last night and early this morning (May 6-7). I followed the text of the play while viewing two different televised productions of it that are posted on Youtube. One is a 1974 production by Britain's Royal Shakespeare Company; the other an American production from 1984. Both had strengths and weaknesses and different cuts to the text in different places, which made having the text at hand a practical necessity.<br /><br />The thing that struck me early on as I watched the play and followed along with the text, was how absorbing the play was from the get-go. And also how much balls-on fun it is, and how sensational and emotionally wringing it is as it proceeds. The advantage of watching two different productions, side by side and back and forth, was seeing how very different the interpretive choices can be, and how each of the approaches was equally valid. In one production, a scene might be downplayed, almost at the level of a hush, while in the other production the same scene is played with high-keyed bluster. Each illuminated the meaning and nuances of the text.<br /><br />It is relevant, I think, to consider the level of knowledge the reader/viewer has of ancient Roman history when tackling this play. I have a considerable interest in it -- though certainly not scholarly expertise -- and knew the basics of this story from other sources, or at least other versions of it, so I had some context and grounding that enhanced the experience. The question is what does Shakespeare bring to this story that is unique? Scholars know the answers to this more than I do, especially as I've only had this one marathon session with it. My thoughts will be random and possibly idiosyncratic -- those of a neophyte who has not consulted the scholarly literature or perceived wisdom.<br /><br />First of all, of course, the play is a love story, but a very complex one; not complex in plotting, per se, but in the confounding and all-too human motivations and cross-purposes harbored in the lovers in the pursuit of that love and in the agendas of those around them as that love plays out.<br /><br />The plays shows a prismatic view of love filtered through the uncertainties borne of human foibles and follies; this is not a *pure* love, and I think this complexity might arise out of Shakespeare's maturity, as this was one of his later plays.<br /><br />Love takes a heavy beating and exacts a heavy toll on everyone in this play; the victims are not just the lovers but entire empires. This great love of the ages sears white hot, then falters and fails, turns to hatred, then rekindles to a molten hotness on the seeming winds of caprice, all at a moments' notice. No matter the rise or fall of their fortunes, of the turns in their paths or the expediencies of momentary politics, these two cannot quit each other. It is an obsession that overcomes all betrayal and political expedience. This makes it very erotic, and very moving.<br /><br />And this is no ordinary love; it is one complicated by the dictates of power. This is love among Gods and demi-gods. Caesar, Cleopatra, and Antony are operating on a plane unknown and inaccessible to all other mortals. They hold the power of life and death in their hands; absolute power makes the heart rage in love all the angrier; the consequences radiate far beyond the bedchamber. The power alone is an aphrodisiacal rush. When the pairing is a power couple, that aphrodisiacal aspect is magnified. It is magnified further still in the realization that this power couple is loving in defiance of the world's most powerful man, Caesar Octavian. This love is a threat to the entire order, as well as to friendships, loyalties and duties.<br /><br />Antony is a capricious man, not a sympathetic character. He is headstrong and irresponsible; he understands duty but is devoted entirely to his passions and self. His indolence in love and his abdication of duty has rightfully infuriated Caesar. And his willful bent toward disaster drives away his most loyal friend, Domitius Enobarbus -- a man who would have his master's back through thick and thin. The manner of Enobarbus' death is Shakespeare at his most sublimely poetic and mysterious. When Enobarbus realizes all is lost, that Antony's dreams of love and conquest have failed utterly, he simply curls up and dies. His friendship, love, and hopes for Antony dissolved, he simply succumbs to dissolution and total annihilation; he sees no other path or alternative. Anything that Antony is, so is Enobarbus; there is no Enobarbus without Antony. Enobarbus is the soul, the conscience of Antony. (He might be said to be The Wisdom of they play, its wise Fool and Greek chorus). The death of one means the death of the other, so inextricably bound are they.<br /><br />This bond between Antony and Enobarbus begs the question of a bisexual reading of the play, because this is not the only manly soul-mating going on in it. Caesar himself seems as emotionally obsessed with Antony as Cleopatra is. What else can account for the Roman leader's constant overlooking of Antony's disloyalty?<br /><br />Cleopatra is a fascinating woman, as realized here; a goddess yet still a player in a man's world. When she willingly bows down to Caesar in her broken state, your heart goes out to her, despite all that she has done. (Indeed, even Caesar can hardly bear to behold a diminished and prostrate Cleopatra; perhaps the falling of a goddess brings home to him an uncomfortable possibility of his own demise. Plus, we see that the magic that captivated Antony might very well capture his heart as well).<br /><br />There's so much more that can be said about this play. Suffice it to say, it's a breathless masterwork; an exciting, infuriating and moving roller-coaster ride of emotion. As always with the Bard, there are meltingly beautiful ponderings in the soliloquies on the fleetingness of life, the caprices of love, the conflicting pulls of social conformity and the inevitable failures inherent in human frailty. <br /><br />I'd like to say a few things about the two performances of the play I watched while reading the text.<br /><br />The first performance is a 1974 British TV production directed by the acclaimed Trevor Nunn, involving the Royal Shakespeare Company. The cast is, of course, impeccable. Richard Johnson is Antony and Janet Suzman, sporting a bit of tan makeup for that Egyptian skintone, is the Queen of the Nile. The cast also includes the late Corin Redgrave as an ice-cold Caesar. Young Patrick Stewart essays Enobarbus in his familiar rich voice. Delightfully, a young Ben Kingsley is Thyreus, the unfortunate messenger who is beaten by Antony as an ineffectual act of vengeance upon Caesar. (In this play, being a messenger boy is the worst possible job; always an invitation to Kingly and Queenly physical abuse). <br /><br />The second performance is a 1984 American TV production that has a mixed British and American cast. This cast is much spottier in quality. Two of the performances are dreadful: Walter Koenig of the original Star Trek series is Pompey, and out of his depth. The other is the elderly John Carradine as the soothsayer, and he is clearly reading cue cards. The great strength, though, of this production, is Timothy Dalton's portrayal of Antony. His Antony is, of course, insanely handsome, brash, loud and cocky, and impertinent, conveying a totally unapologetic air and selfishness in his passions. Lynn Redgrave makes for a clear, articulate and very good Cleopatra.<br /><br />Both productions have strengths and weaknesses, and I can't recommend one over the other and would recommend both be seen. The British version is more "cinematic," with more production polish and better acting. The American is "stagier" but keeps more of the text. In fact, the British production cuts the play text rather too heavily, in my opinion.<br /><br />The Antony, Cleopatra and Caesar in both productions are all very good, providing quite striking contrasts in how to approach these characters and their lines.<br /><br />Richard Johnson's Antony seethes with a quiet gruffness and world weariness. He is not "sexy" or glamorous as one might expect Antony to be, though, in the way Dalton is, but he exudes an inexorable and formidable authority. Timothy Dalton, by contrast is a whirlwind Antony, loud and boisterous, booming his lines to the heavens.<br /><br />Janet Suzman's Cleopatra purrs and smolders breathily while Lynn Redgraves minces and essays a bell-clear sharp tongue, providing more of the business-like mien of the character. I like both approaches but actually prefer Redgrave's in this case.<br /><br />Anthony Geary (yes, Luke, of General Hospital) actually makes for a credible Caesar in the American production. He is doubtless the king, but he is also prone to vulnerability. Corin Redgrave in the British production is a sneering, granitic and frighteningly lizard-like Caesar not to be messed with.<br /><br />Both actors performing Enobarbus are good. Beyond that the American cast becomes inferior.<br /><br />Although I still consider <i>King Lear</i> to be the pinnacle of the Bard's art, I am seriously thinking that <i>Antony and Cleopatra</i> for sheer entertainment value and emotional power is my new favorite Shakespeare play.<br /><br />I will be looking to see if this year's Shakespeare in the Park program includes a staging. I am anxious to go.<br /><br />(KevinR@Ky 2016)</span>
  <a data-text-id="9301867806824215783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_105580401" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105580401" />
    <a title="Rowland Pasaribu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3564385-rowland-pasaribu"><img alt="Rowland Pasaribu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513834488p2/3564385.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105580401?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowland Pasaribu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowland Pasaribu" href="/user/show/3564385-rowland-pasaribu">Rowland Pasaribu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780192834256" href="/book/show/295475.Anthony_and_Cleopatra">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105580401" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14061593031826772819">Scholars believe that Shakespeare wrote Antony and Cleopatra in 1606, immediately after Macbeth, and it is one of the last great tragedies that Shakespeare produced. The most geographically sweeping of Shakespeare’s plays, Antony and Cleopatra’s setting is the entire Roman Empire, its backdrop the well-documented history of Octavius Caesar, Marc Antony, and Cleopatra. Shakespeare’s primary source for Antony and Cleopatra was the Life of Marcus Antonius contained in Plutarch’s Lives of the Noble </span>
  <span id="freeText14061593031826772819" style="display:none">Scholars believe that Shakespeare wrote Antony and Cleopatra in 1606, immediately after Macbeth, and it is one of the last great tragedies that Shakespeare produced. The most geographically sweeping of Shakespeare’s plays, Antony and Cleopatra’s setting is the entire Roman Empire, its backdrop the well-documented history of Octavius Caesar, Marc Antony, and Cleopatra. Shakespeare’s primary source for Antony and Cleopatra was the Life of Marcus Antonius contained in Plutarch’s Lives of the Noble Grecians and Romans, which was translated into English by Sir Thomas North in 1579. North’s language was so rich that Shakespeare incorporated large, relatively unchanged excerpts of it into his text. The plot of the play also remains close to North’s history, although characters like Enobarbus and Cleopatra’s attendants are largely Shakespearean creations.<br /><br />The action of the story takes place roughly two years after the events of Shakespeare’s earlier play about the Roman Empire, Julius Caesar. At the beginning of that tragedy, Caesar has triumphed over his rival Pompey the Great, the father of young Pompey in Antony and Cleopatra, and aspires to kingship. Caesar is then assassinated by Cassius and Brutus, who hope to preserve the Roman Republic. Instead, Cassius and Brutus are defeated by Mark Antony and Octavius Caesar, Julius’s nephew, who then join Marcus Aemilius Lepidus to create a three-man government, or triumvirate, over the empire.<br /><br />Historically, the action of Antony and Cleopatra takes place over a ten-year span, whereas in the play the story is compressed to fit the needs of the stage. Antony is clearly much older than he was in Julius Caesar, and his political instincts seem to be waning. Octavius Caesar was only a minor character in the earlier play, but here he comes into his own as the man who will rise to become the first Roman emperor. Most of the political battles and machinations depicted are historically accurate, as is the romance of the title characters.<br /><br />In his opening lines to Demetrius, Philo complains that Antony has abandoned the military endeavors on which his reputation is based for Cleopatra’s sake. His criticism of Antony’s “dotage,” or stupidity, introduces a tension between reason and emotion that runs throughout the play (I.i.1). Antony and Cleopatra’s first exchange heightens this tension, as they argue whether their love can be put into words and understood or whether it exceeds such faculties and boundaries of reason. If, according to Roman consensus, Antony is the military hero and disciplined statesmen that Caesar and others believe him to be, then he seems to have happily abandoned his reason in order to pursue his passion. He declares: “Let Rome in Tiber melt, and the wide arch / Of the ranged empire fall” (I.i.35–36). The play, however, is more concerned with the battle between reason and emotion than the triumph of one over the other, and this battle is waged most forcefully in the character of Antony. More than any other character in the play, Antony vacillates between Western and Eastern sensibilities, feeling pulled by both his duty to the empire and his desire for pleasure, his want of military glory and his passion for Cleopatra. Soon after his nonchalant dismissal of Caesar’s messenger, the empire, and his duty to it, he chastises himself for his neglect and commits to return to Rome, lest he “lose [him:]self in dotage” (I.ii.106).<br /><br />As the play progresses, Antony continues to inhabit conflicting identities that play out the struggle between reason and emotion. At one moment, he is the vengeful war hero whom Caesar praises and fears. Soon thereafter, he sacrifices his military position by unwisely allowing Cleopatra to determine his course of action. As his Roman allies—even the ever-faithful Enobarbus—abandon him, Antony feels that he has, indeed, lost himself in dotage, and he determines to rescue his noble identity by taking his own life. At first, this course of action may appear to be a triumph of reason over passion, of -Western sensibilities over Eastern ones, but the play is not that simple. Although Antony dies believing himself a man of honor, discipline, and reason, our understanding of him is not nearly as straight-forward. In order to come to terms with Antony’s character, we must analyze the aspects of his identity that he ignores. He is, in the end, a man ruled by passion as much as by reason. Likewise, the play offers us a worldview in which one sensibility cannot easily dominate another. Reason cannot ever fully conquer the passions, nor can passion wholly undo reason.<br /><br />Although Antony and Cleopatra details the conflict between Rome and Egypt, giving us an idea of the Elizabethan perceptions of the difference between Western and Eastern cultures, it does not make a definitive statement about which culture ultimately triumphs. In the play, the Western and Eastern poles of the world are characterized by those who inhabit them: Caesar, for instance, embodies the stoic duty of the West, while Cleopatra, in all her theatrical grandeur, represents the free-flowing passions of the East. Caesar’s concerns throughout the play are certainly imperial: he means to invade foreign lands in order to invest them with traditions and sensibilities of his own. But the play resists siding with this imperialist impulse. Shakespeare, in other words, does not align the play’s sympathies with the West; Antony and Cleopatra can hardly be read as propaganda for Western domination. On the contrary, the Roman understanding of Cleopatra and her kingdom seems exceedingly superficial. To Caesar, the queen of Egypt is little more than a whore with a flair for drama. His perspective allows little room for the real power of Cleopatra’s sexuality—she can, after all, persuade the most decorated of generals to follow her into ignoble retreat. Similarly, it allows little room for the indomitable strength of her will, which she demonstrates so forcefully at the end of the play as she refuses to allow herself to be turned into a “Egyptian puppet” for the entertainment of the Roman masses (V.ii.204).<br /><br />In Antony and Cleopatra, West meets East, but it does not, regardless of Caesar’s triumph over the land of Egypt, conquer it. Cleopatra’s suicide suggests that something of the East’s spirit, the freedoms and passions that are not represented in the play’s conception of the West, cannot be subsumed by Caesar’s victory. The play suggests that the East will live on as a visible and unconquerable counterpoint to the West, bound as inseparably and eternally as Antony and Cleopatra are in their tomb.<br /><br />In one sentence, i will say this is, "The Struggle Between Reason and Emotion"… :D<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14061593031826772819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer15890780804454243224">Not what I expected at all. A Midsummer Night's Dream is much more exotic than this classic love story between a Roman man and an Egyptian woman. The play should really be called Antony and Caesar because it's more about their relationship than it is about Antony and Cleopatra's. The introduction in my edition notes that there are many flaws in this play and that it's too long. I agree.   <span id="freeText15890780804454243224" style="display:none">Not what I expected at all. A Midsummer Night's Dream is much more exotic than this classic love story between a Roman man and an Egyptian woman. The play should really be called Antony and Caesar because it's more about their relationship than it is about Antony and Cleopatra's. The introduction in my edition notes that there are many flaws in this play and that it's too long. I agree. This doesn't have Shakespeare's usual humor or eloquence, and there are many short short scenes that could have (should have) been cut. My intro also states that this play isn't very popular and hasn't been over the years, that others' versions of this story have done better, and I understand why. Honestly, this is my least favorite Shakespeare play so far, and that includes Two Gentlemen of Verona. That, at least, was funny! </span>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2497318">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_306095738" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306095738" />
    <a title="R.J. Askew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8367297-r-j-askew"><img alt="R.J. Askew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419287673p2/8367297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/306095738?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="R.J. Askew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="R.J. Askew" href="/user/show/8367297-r-j-askew">R.J. Askew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780192834256" href="/book/show/295475.Anthony_and_Cleopatra">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer306095738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7720836528212828663">A WONDERFUL PIECE OF WORK<br/> <br/><br/> Love. Power. Love. Power. Which is the eye drawn to? It’s said women love powerful men. So does love follow power? Wealth seems to. Are powerful men happier than all the rest? And powerful women? It’s said that men are terrified of them. And if the lover loves the power more than its holder? Can love conquor power? And if it does? What of power then? Can a powerful man surrender to love and remain powerful? Behind every powerful man… Power. Love. Power. Love. Antony. C</span>
  <span id="freeText7720836528212828663" style="display:none">A WONDERFUL PIECE OF WORK<br /> <br /><br /> Love. Power. Love. Power. Which is the eye drawn to? It’s said women love powerful men. So does love follow power? Wealth seems to. Are powerful men happier than all the rest? And powerful women? It’s said that men are terrified of them. And if the lover loves the power more than its holder? Can love conquor power? And if it does? What of power then? Can a powerful man surrender to love and remain powerful? Behind every powerful man… Power. Love. Power. Love. Antony. Cleo. Antony. Cleo.<br /><br /> ‘Would I had never seen her!’ says Mark Antony. <br /> But he does, and becomes in Rome’s eye, ‘the bellows and the fan to cool a gipsy’s lust,’ and, ‘The triple pillar of the world transform’d into a strumpet’s fool…’<br /> But then she is ‘…a wonderful piece of work…’, as Antony’s supporter Enobarbus says. <br /> And she herself says, ‘I was a morsel for a monarch’.<br /> ‘Let witchcraft join with beauty, lust with both’, says Pompey’s son.<br /> ‘She is cunning past man’s thought,’ says Antony.<br /> Musing on fishing, she says, ‘…my bended hook shall pierce their slimy jaws…I’ll think them every one an Antony, And say, ‘Ah ha!’ you’re caught.’<br /> And he is.<br /> On being entertained on her sumptuous barge he ‘…pays with his heart for what his eyes eat only.’<br /> Such is her ‘infinite variety’.<br /> <br /> Rome is power, the greatest there is. Power brings conquest, treasure, honour. Power is all. Power instinctively grows more powerful, loves power.<br /> Rome has been a republic for five hundred years and is governed by a senatorial class of aristocratic families and tribunes elected by the people – the plebians. <br /> But the power massed in the hands of men like Julius Caesar – warrior politicians – becomes too great for the old order to curb.<br /> Julius Caesar, aided by Antony – a popular general, becomes dictator of Rome. This is the fulcrum in Rome’s history. The crisis deepens when the old order kills Caesar.<br /> Antony takes revenge on the murders and joins a new ruling clique – the triumvirate – with the young Octavius Caesar, Julius Caesar’s adopted son and heir, and Lepidus, another of Julius Caesar’s allies.<br /> Antony knows the hardships of war, and relates to common soldiers, the ‘lads’, with whom he is on familiar terms. He lives loose, drinks deep, and makes free with other mens’ wives.<br /> The luxury of Egypt, in his third of the Roman world, is irresistible to him, as is its famous queen.<br /> Cleopatra was the lover of Julius Caesar, with whom she had a son, Caesarion, and Caesar’s great rival, Pompey.<br /> If Rome is power, order, reason, Egypt is pleasure, ease, romance.<br /><br /> ‘…we did sleep day out…and make night light with drinking…’ says Enobarbus, adding later, ‘…we have used our throats in Egypt.’<br /> Did you really have eight wild boar roasted whole at a breakfast? asks one of Caesar’s friends.<br /><br /> Enter a messenger.<br /><br /> But Mark Antony can’t forget Rome. Messengers fly back and forth. Antony’s brother and wife, Fulvia, war with Caesar in Italy, in part ‘to have me out of Egypt’, Antony says.<br /> ‘A Roman thought hath struck him,’ Cleopatra mocks.<br /> She tries to beguile him by pretending to be sad if he is merry and merry if he is sad.<br /> But Rome tugs at Antony. ‘Our Italy shines o’er with civil swords,’ he says. ‘I must be gone. These strong Egyptian fetters I must break.’<br /><br /> Enter another messenger.<br /><br /> But first a little bawdy joking among Cleopatra’s ladies and a soothsayer who tells Charmian, ‘You shall outlive the lady whom you serve.’ Such is Egypt.<br /><br /> Enter a messenger.<br /><br /> Fulvia is dead. And Pompey the younger challenges the triumvirate. Caesar says ‘…we do bear so great weight in his lightness’ of Antony’s absence.<br /> But then he is in Rome and it is Cleopatra who misses him ‘…does he walk? Or is he on his horse? O happy, horse, to bear the weight of Antony!’<br /> Antony the politician makes up with Caesar who marries his sister, Octavia, to him.<br /> But Enobarbus says, ‘He will to his Egyptian dish again,’ and predicts that the marriage will later ‘prove the immediate author of their variance.’<br /><br /> Strikes him down.<br /><br /> Cleopatra is enraged at the hapless messenger who brings news of the marriage. ‘Thou shalt be whipp’d with wire, and stewed in brine.’<br /><br /> On board Pompey’s galley.<br /> <br /> Lepidius, drunk, asks, ‘What manner o’thing is your crocodile?’ Antony mocks him, ‘It is shaped sir, like itself…’<br /> Pompey declines an offer from a pirate to murder the triumvirs on his behalf as this would dishonour him. The pirate deserts him. Ventidius, a subordinate of Antony’s later says he did not do as much while fighting the parthains as he could have done, so as not to, ‘acquire too high a fame’. Lepidius, the weakest triumvir, is however caught between his love for Caesar and his adoration of Antony. <br /> Will Caesar weep when he parts from Octavia? Antony ‘cried almost to roaring’ on Julius Caesar’s death.<br /> ‘Is she as tall as me? What majesty is in her gait?’ asks Cleopatra. ‘She creeps,’ says the now wily messenger.<br /><br /> The play is riddled contrasts between rivals and contrasts within in the principals, much as Plutrach’s Lives, Shakespeare’s source for this and his other Roman plays, contrasts the lives of noble Greeks and Romans and strengths and weakness of character in individuals. There are two sides to everything. Life is comedy and tragedy. <br />---------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br /> We are now almost at the mid-point of Act III and the fulcrum of the play, after which events spin out of Mark Antony’s control.<br /><br />---------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br /> Antony complains that Caesar is at war with Pompey and has deposed Lepidus. Octavia is distraught, torn, like Lepidus, between praying for her husband Antony and her brother Octavius, saying there is, ‘…no midway twix these extrmes at all.’<br /> Caesar is angered thatm ‘Cleopatra and himself in chairs of gold were publicly enthron’d’. <br /> ‘He hath given his empire up to a whore; who now are levying the kings o’the earth for war.’<br /> <br /> A plain near Actium.<br /><br /> Enobarbus the soldier, exasperated by Cleopatra’s presence, moans, ‘But why, why, why?’ He adds, ‘…if we should serve with horse and mares together…your presence must puzzle Antony…’<br /> ‘Sink Rome,’ snaps Cleopatra.<br /> Antony is stronger on land than at sea and a common soldier pleads, ‘ O noble emperor! Do not fight by sea…’ And Canidius, who later deserts Antony, says, ‘…our leader’s led, and we are women’s men.’<br /> But Anony fights Ceasar at sea and is undone when Cleopatra flees the battle with her sixty ships, ‘like a cow in June’, and Antony follows, ‘like a doting mallard’.<br /> Scarus says, ‘Experience, manhood, honour, ne’er before did violate itself so.’<br /> <br /> ‘Love, I am full of lead,’ says Antony after, perhaps addressing the concept of love as well as Cleopatra. My heart was tied to your rudder, he tells her.<br /> He is reduced to sending his old teacher, Euphronius, to treat with his younger rival, such is his impotence.<br /> ‘What shall we do, Enobarbus?’ asks Cleopatra.<br /> ‘Think, and die,’ comes the reply.<br /> Antony is now so desperate he challenges Caesar to fight him, ‘sword against sword’.<br /> Enobarbus in a scornful aside says Caesar triumphant will not risk all, ‘Against a sworder!’ and in another aside says, ‘Sir, sir, thou’rt so leaky, that we must leave thee to thy sinking...'<br /> Antony has Caesar’s messenger whipped and rails against Cleopatra, ‘I found you as a morsel, cold upon dead Caesar’s trencher…’ She calls down poisoned hailstones on herself.<br /> Antony rallies, ‘Let’s have one other gaudy night…’<br /> Enobarbus sees it all, ‘Now he’ll outstare the lightning. A diminution in our captain’s brain restores his heart. I will seek some other way to leave him.’<br /> ‘He calls me boy,’ says Caesar.<br /><br /> ‘Tend me tonight two hours,’ Antony bids of his servants, as if expecting doom. Soldiers hear, ‘Music i’ the air’, and suspect it marks his favourite god, Hercules, deserting him.<br /> Cleopatra helps him on with his armour like a fussing wife and he kisses her, ‘This is a soldier’s kiss.’ This is their only kiss in the play’s text.<br /> Later, when things go well for him, he refers to her as, ‘My nightingale…’ Previously he says fondly, ‘Where’s my serpent of old Nile?’ And he sends her a pearl from Rome, at which point he was to her ‘man of men’ and she was writing to him every day, saying to her maid,‘Ink and paper, Charmain.’<br /><br /> When Enobarbus finally deserts him, Antony, sees the consequences of his mistakes, ‘O! my fortunes have corrupted honest men.’ Enobarbus rues his desertion saying of himself, ‘I am alone the villain of the earth.’ When Antony’s fortunes rally briefly – pointedly in a land battle – Enobarbus dies, disconsolate, ‘A master-leaver and a fugitive.’<br /> But then, ‘All is lost!’ during a second sea battle and Antony calls Cleopatra a ‘triple-turn’d whore!’ and says she ‘Hast sold me to this novice,’ Caesar.<br /> At Actium, one of Antony’s soldiers called her, ‘Yon ribaudred nag of Egypt’ and Antony now calls her ‘a right gypsy…fast and loose’, as if coming round to the Roman view of her as ‘gypsy’ in the play’s opening lines. <br /> He calls her ‘the greatest spot of all thy sex’, talks of Caesar hoisting her up ‘to the shouting plebians’, and says ‘The witch shall die: to the young Roman boy she hath sold me’.<br /><br /> Terrified by Antony’s rage, Cleopatra bolts to her monument and sends her eunuch to say she is dead.<br /> Antony, ‘Dead then?’ Eunuch, ‘Dead.’<br /> The news drives Antony to tell his man Eros to kill him. But Eros kills himself instead. Antony, shamed by Eros, falls on his own sword, but fails to kill himself.<br /> ‘Let him that loves me strike me dead,’ he commands of his guards. But they all refuse. <br /> He is taken to Cleopatra’s monument. ‘I am dying, Egypt, dying.’<br /> Cleopatra and her maids hoist him up. ‘How heavy weight my lord!’ she says. ‘Our strength is goint into heaviness, that makes the weight.’<br /> The point is hammered home when all the women say, ‘A heavy sight!’ And Antony repeats, ‘I am dying Egypt, dying.’ <br /><br /> Antony dies.<br /><br /> In the final act Caesar, ‘sole sir o’the world,’ makes his plans and Cleopatra treats with him. She dreams of Antony and speaks of him as a generous and outstanding man, but not in a romantic way, as a lover.<br /> Caesar seems reasonable yet threatens her children if she does not comply. She then discovers his true intent is to parade her to through Rome ‘in triumph.’<br /> Yet, she too remains slippery, disguising her true wealth from Caesar.<br /> She is in horror of the Roman mob with their ‘greasy aprons…thick breaths…rank of gross diet, shall we be enclouded, and forc’d to drink their vapour?’<br /> There seems more passion in this fear than in her dream of Antony, though Shakespeare was surely trying to make his own smelly audience think of themselves as Romans.<br /> And so, to join Antony and foil Caesar, she applies an asp. But first she kisses one of her maids, who promptly dies. She applies a second asp, dies. Another maid applies an asp, dies. <br /> Octavius Caesar, powerless to stop Cleopatra’s bid for immortality, goes on to rule Rome for decades and make good his comment that, ‘The time of universal peace is near.’<br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br /> Power wins. Love for its own sake is nowhere. Cleopatra’s love of Antony’s power and Antony’s sensuousness permit her to cloak his power as a rampant ivy cloaks a tree until said tree falls, taking said ivy with it. Antony speaks with more passion about his honour, or hurtfully of Cleopatra when his cause fails, than he ever does in a romantic way to or about her. And she remains guileful to the end and as concerned with her royalty and spiting Caesar’s plan to show her off as she is about joining Antony in death. But the ‘boy’ Caesar is immune to her ways and does not even recognise her when they first meet. We also know from Plutarch that Cleopatra tested methods of suicide on prisoners to discover the gentlest, a woman who left little to chance.<br /> But are things ever so simple? The complexity of all things is captured when Cleopatra says to her first asp, ‘This knot intrinsicate of life at once untie’. The joining of ‘intrinsic’ and ‘intricate’ in the portmanteau word ‘intrinsicate’ tells us that life is never one thing. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7720836528212828663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72457313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16333506865055643921">Their celebrity couple name would be... AntoPatra.<br/><br/>And essentially, that is what Antony and Cleopatra are: a celebrity couple. And just like the crazy jump-on-the-couch love of TomKat, their affection is subject to deep suspicion and speculation by the public. The romance of AntoPatra is often compared with that of Romeo and Juliet in regard to both passion and poetry. However, the circumstance of celebrity makes Antony and Cleopatra vastly, sadly different from the star-crossed young lovers of V</span>
  <span id="freeText16333506865055643921" style="display:none">Their celebrity couple name would be... AntoPatra.<br /><br />And essentially, that is what Antony and Cleopatra are: a celebrity couple. And just like the crazy jump-on-the-couch love of TomKat, their affection is subject to deep suspicion and speculation by the public. The romance of AntoPatra is often compared with that of Romeo and Juliet in regard to both passion and poetry. However, the circumstance of celebrity makes Antony and Cleopatra vastly, sadly different from the star-crossed young lovers of Verona. It is tantamount to comparing Brangelina with some high school sweethearts from Nowhere, Montana. Celebrities must adjust their behaviors and passions to suit their positions. The most interesting aspect of AntoPatra's love is therefore not the expression of their affection, but rather the adjustment of their amour.<br /><br />These lovers seem to have torn a page from a Harold Pinter play and mastered the art of subtext, saying more between their lines than within them. This is especially the case when other characters are witness to the exchanges between the lovers. A celebrity couple is never truly alone, since they belong, at least in part, to the public. The fact that this is a play and an audience is truly present (even during "private" moments) just serves to elevate that paradox. For example, in Romeo and Juliet, the audience may often find itself holding its collective breath as it attempts not to interrupt the intimacy of the incredibly private trysts of the young lovers. On the other hand, in Antony and Cleopatra, the audience is the literal translation, an extension, of the public eye characterized onstage. Every cough and shuffle of the audience can just serve to remind Antony and Cleopatra that someone, somewhere, is always watching.<br /><br />When a celebrity relationship first develops, we all conjecture over whether it's "for real." Is it just because his career is slacking? Is it a stunt to take media attention off her botched boob job? Etc. Etc. Ad Nauseum. And the more I view AntoPatra as a celebrity couple, the more I find myself analyzing the sincerity of their purported affection. Would Cleopatra love Antony if he were a mere Roman warrior? Would Antony care for Cleopatra if she were an Egyptian peasant? Likely not. I'll go out on a limb here and say defiantly not. While it can't be said that their love is based solely on the advantages one garners for the other (their relationship often creates more drama than glory), it is likely that they love based on the prestige of their partner. They see each other as worthy, powerful equals. As with modern celebrity couples, the attraction of power and wealth to power and wealth isn't necessarily just due to elitism. They understand (even if they fail to accept) the constraints that power, wealth and position imply. (Complete review available at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.whatrefuge.blogspot.com">www.whatrefuge.blogspot.com</a>)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16333506865055643921" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/483141827?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rhiannon Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rhiannon Johnson" href="/user/show/6458878-rhiannon-johnson">Rhiannon Johnson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer483141827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10989610415697783854">The character Cleopatra, in William Shakespeare's “Antony and Cleopatra,” possesses a multitude of contradictions. Through constant clashes in speech and action, Shakespeare constructs a complex female character. Critic Anna Jameson refers to Cleopatra as “a brilliant antithesis—a compound of contradictions” (Quint 244). Jameson recognizes Shakespeare's “deep meaning and wonderous [sic] skill in the apparent enigma” of Cleopatra (244). Shakespeare remediates the stories of Plutarch and Genesis t</span>
  <span id="freeText10989610415697783854" style="display:none">The character Cleopatra, in William Shakespeare's “Antony and Cleopatra,” possesses a multitude of contradictions. Through constant clashes in speech and action, Shakespeare constructs a complex female character. Critic Anna Jameson refers to Cleopatra as “a brilliant antithesis—a compound of contradictions” (Quint 244). Jameson recognizes Shakespeare's “deep meaning and wonderous [sic] skill in the apparent enigma” of Cleopatra (244). Shakespeare remediates the stories of Plutarch and Genesis to give agency to his character. Through appropriation Shakespeare shapes his “literary forbears to new uses, enhancing,extending, or critiquing the meaning of the primary text” (Savu 22). Through remediation, Shakespeare emphasizes Cleopatra's sexual power, and shifts the image of the snake from a male to a female symbol of power, in order to give agency to Cleopatra in her suicide.<br /><br />To read my paper "Remediating Cleopatra" visit:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://writerrhiannon.blogspot.com/2012/12/antony-and-cleopatra.html">http://writerrhiannon.blogspot.com/20...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10989610415697783854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;

    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fU7No+keasHzc9djVUYykhcBjuu/0/ladZ5vKwaCPvxKgx45PTZVJQP8xcTyS5hYMzOZlVdzvY5m1ent6ZcVTQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lZTbBhrzgXzizd30qW6TpNPRldY9FoAEvM9bn/MCHdaiWQicztu+mBJCz1MOYzlu9+OCqNW2xNCvhN1ZHBc2Zw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

But that year, at age forty-two, he found his footing again, finishing a play he had begun the previous autumn—King Lear—then writing two other great tragedies, Macbeth and Antony and Cleopatra.It was a memorable year in England as well—and a grim one, in the aftermath of a terrorist plot conceived by a small group of Catholic gentry that had been uncovered at the last hour. The foiled Gunpowder Plot would have blown up the king and royal family along with the nation’s political and religious leadership. The aborted plot renewed anti-Catholic sentiment and laid bare divisions in the kingdom.It was against this background that Shakespeare finished Lear, a play about a divided kingdom, then wrote a tragedy that turned on the murder of a Scottish king, Macbeth. He ended this astonishing year with a third masterpiece no less steeped in current events and concerns: Antony and Cleopatra.The Year of Lear sheds light on these three great tragedies by placing them in the context of their times, while also allowing us greater insight into how Shakespeare was personally touched by such events as a terrible outbreak of plague and growing religious divisions.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare">About William Shakespeare</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1586700347p3/947.jpg);'></div>

</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10787146052860524931">William Shakespeare (baptised 26 April 1564) was an English poet and playwright, widely regarded as the greatest writer in the English language and the world's pre-eminent dramatist. He is often called England's national poet and the "Bard of Avon" (or simply "The Bard"). His surviving works consist of 38 plays, 154 sonnets, two long narrative poems, and several other poems. His plays have been tr</span>
  <span id="freeText10787146052860524931" style="display:none">William Shakespeare (baptised 26 April 1564) was an English poet and playwright, widely regarded as the greatest writer in the English language and the world's pre-eminent dramatist. He is often called England's national poet and the "Bard of Avon" (or simply "The Bard"). His surviving works consist of 38 plays, 154 sonnets, two long narrative poems, and several other poems. His plays have been translated into every major living language, and are performed more often than those of any other playwright.<br /><br />Shakespeare was born and raised in Stratford-upon-Avon. Scholars believe that he died on his fifty-second birthday, coinciding with St George’s Day.<br /><br />At the age of 18 he married Anne Hathaway, who bore him three children: Susanna, and twins Hamnet and Judith. Between 1585 and 1592 he began a successful career in London as an actor, writer, and part owner of the playing company the Lord Chamberlain's Men, later known as the King's Men. He appears to have retired to Stratford around 1613, where he died three years later. Few records of Shakespeare's private life survive, and there has been considerable speculation about such matters as his sexuality, religious beliefs, and whether the works attributed to him were written by others.<br /><br />Shakespeare produced most of his known work between 1590 and 1613. His early plays were mainly comedies and histories, genres he raised to the peak of sophistication and artistry by the end of the sixteenth century. Next he wrote mainly tragedies until about 1608, including <i>Hamlet, King Lear</i>, and <i>Macbeth</i>, considered some of the finest examples in the English language. In his last phase, he wrote tragicomedies, also known as romances, and collaborated with other playwrights. Many of his plays were published in editions of varying quality and accuracy during his lifetime, and in 1623, two of his former theatrical colleagues published the <i>First Folio</i>, a collected edition of his dramatic works that included all but two of the plays now recognised as Shakespeare's.<br /><br />Shakespeare was a respected poet and playwright in his own day, but his reputation did not rise to its present heights until the nineteenth century. The Romantics, in particular, acclaimed Shakespeare's genius, and the Victorians hero-worshipped Shakespeare with a reverence that George Bernard Shaw called "bardolatry". In the twentieth century, his work was repeatedly adopted and rediscovered by new movements in scholarship and performance. His plays remain highly popular today and are consistently performed and reinterpreted in diverse cultural and political contexts throughout the world.<br /><br />According to historians, Shakespeare wrote 37 plays and 154 sonnets throughout the span of his life. Shakespeare's writing average was 1.5 plays a year since he first started writing in 1589. There have been plays and sonnets attributed to Shakespeare that were not authentically written by the great master of language and literature. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10787146052860524931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
